# Literature Review: What causes hallucinations in large language models?

## Literature Review

**What drives LLMs to generate “made‑up” statements?**  A growing body of work points to three intertwined sources: (1) systematic biases inherited from the pre‑training corpus, (2) the model’s internal uncertainty and the way it is expressed during decoding, and (3) fundamental statistical‑theoretic limits of autoregressive generation.  Below we synthesize the most salient findings, grouping them by these themes rather than by chronology.

**Pre‑training artefacts and memorisation.**  Empirical behavioural studies on LLaMA, GPT‑3.5 and PaLM reveal that hallucinations often arise from two predictable pre‑training biases.  First, models memorise whole sentences and later retrieve them as “indices” when a hypothesis matches any memorised fragment, leading them to label an NLI pair as entailed even when the premise is unrelated [McKenna et al., 2023].  Second, statistical frequency cues—such as a predicate that is rarer in the training data than its counterpart—bias the model toward asserting entailment for the more common predicate, regardless of logical validity [McKenna et al., 2023].  Similar conclusions emerge from adversarial‑attack experiments that show weak‑semantic and out‑of‑distribution prompts exacerbate these biases, especially when the prompt is ambiguous or the training data are sparse [Tripathi et al., 2024; Kevin, 2026].

**Predictive uncertainty and internal‑state signatures.**  Hallucination correlates strongly with epistemic uncertainty.  In conditional generation tasks (image captioning, data‑to‑text) higher predictive uncertainty—particularly epistemic rather than aleatoric—predicts a greater likelihood of factual errors [Xiao & Wang, 2021].  Probing the hidden activations of LLMs confirms that specific neurons, layers and token‑level activations encode whether a query has been seen during training and whether the model expects to hallucinate [Ji et al., 2024].  A lightweight probing estimator can predict hallucination risk with >84 % accuracy at inference time, suggesting that internal‑state monitoring is a viable diagnostic tool [Ji et al., 2024].  Complementary work that analyses attention‑weight patterns in ChatGPT and Gemini also finds distinct “hallucination‑prone” configurations that can be detected via anomaly‑detection pipelines [Zablocki & Gajewska, 2024].

**Theoretical inevitability and trade‑offs.**  Several mathematical investigations argue that hallucination is not merely a bug but an unavoidable by‑product of lossy compression and stochastic sampling.  A parametric analysis of GPT‑style models shows a formal trade‑off between creativity (high‑entropy sampling) and factual fidelity, with an optimal balance that still permits non‑zero hallucination [Lee, 2023].  Information‑theoretic arguments further claim that any sufficiently intelligent generative system must perform lossy abstraction, making “un‑grounded divergence” inevitable [Kim, 2025; Kim, 2025 (geometric)]; the curvature of the semantic manifold predicts hallucination rates independent of data density [Kim, 2025 (geometric)].  Stochastic‑process models that treat hallucination as a Markov transition between factual and fictional states achieve modest performance gains but confirm that the phenomenon is intrinsic to the probabilistic dynamics of autoregressive decoding [Sanghavi & Jawale, 2026].

**Implications for mitigation and future directions.**  While the above works highlight root causes, they also suggest practical levers.  Retrieval‑augmented generation can reduce knowledge‑driven hallucination by grounding outputs in external evidence, yet errors in query formulation or document selection can re‑introduce bias [Yang, 2026; Berti et al., 2025].  Prompt‑design studies demonstrate that system instructions and few‑shot examples suppress hallucination more effectively than temperature scaling [Kabakuş, 2026], and that self‑reflection or refusal strategies (e.g., “I don’t know”) trade precision for safety [Saha et al., 2025].  Finally, the “knowledge‑overshadowing” effect—where strong internal priors drown out retrieved facts—has been formalised as a measurable bias and can be mitigated by calibrated consensus mechanisms such as Epistemic Field Theory [Hamzah & Teng, 2026].  Together, these strands suggest a multi‑level research agenda: (i) curating training corpora to minimise memorisation shortcuts, (ii) building internal‑state monitors for uncertainty‑aware decoding, and (iii) developing principled, theory‑grounded frameworks that acknowledge hallucination as an inherent statistical limit while engineering safeguards around it.
